class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy<span id="Etymology"></span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Toponymy_of_England" title="Toponymy of England">Toponymy of England</a></div>\n<p>The name "England" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> name <i>Englaland</i>, which means "land of the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The Angles were one of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> that settled in Great Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>. The Angles came from the <a href="/wiki/Anglia_(peninsula)" title="Anglia (peninsula)">Anglia</a> peninsula in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Kiel" title="Bay of Kiel">Bay of Kiel</a> area (present-day German state of <a href="/wiki/Schleswig%E2%80%93Holstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Schleswig\xe2\x80\x93Holstein">Schleswig\xe2\x80\x93Holstein</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The earliest recorded use of the term, as "Engla londe", is in the late-ninth-century translation into <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_History_of_the_English_People" title="Ecclesiastical History of the English People">Ecclesiastical History of the English People</a></i>. The term was then used in a different sense to the modern one, meaning "the land inhabited by the English", and it included English people in what is now south-east Scotland but was then part of the English kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> recorded that the <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a> of 1086 covered the whole of England, meaning the English kingdom, but a few years later the <i>Chronicle</i> stated that King <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Malcolm III">Malcolm III</a> went "out of Scotlande into <a href="/wiki/Lothian" title="Lothian">Lothian</a> in Englaland", thus using it in the more ancient sense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMolyneaux20156\xe2\x80\x937_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMolyneaux20156\xe2\x80\x937-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>, its modern spelling was first used in 1538.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest attested reference to the Angles occurs in the 1st-century work by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>Anglii</i> is used.<sup id="cite_ref-Fordham_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The etymology of the tribal name itself is disputed by scholars; it has been suggested that it derives from the shape of the Angeln peninsula, an <i>angular</i> shape.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> How and why a term derived from the name of a tribe that was less significant than others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, came to be used for the entire country and its people is not known, but it seems this is related to the custom of calling the Germanic people in Britain <i>Angli Saxones</i> or English Saxons to distinguish them from continental Saxons (Eald-Seaxe) of Old Saxony between Weser and Eider rivers in Northern Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, another language which developed on the island of Great Britain, the Saxon tribe gave their name to the word for England (<i>Sasunn</i>);<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> similarly, the Welsh name for the English language is "<i>Saesneg</i>".\n</p><p>An alternative name for England is <a href="/wiki/Albion" title="Albion">Albion</a>. The name <i>Albion</i> originally referred to the entire island of Great Britain. The nominally earliest record of the name appears in the <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Aristotelicum" title="Corpus Aristotelicum">Corpus</a>, specifically the 4th-century BC <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Cosmos" class="mw-redirect" title="On the Cosmos">De Mundo</a></i>:<sup id="cite_ref-massey_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-massey-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> "Beyond the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a> is the ocean that flows round the earth. In it are two very large islands called Britannia; these are Albion and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ierne</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-massey_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-massey-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ArOntheCosmos_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArOntheCosmos-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> But modern scholarly consensus ascribes <i>De Mundo</i> not to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> but to <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Aristotle" title="Pseudo-Aristotle">Pseudo-Aristotle</a>, i.e. it was written later in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Graeco-Roman</a> period or afterwards. The word <i>Albion</i> (\xe1\xbc\x88\xce\xbb\xce\xb2\xce\xaf\xcf\x89\xce\xbd) or <i>insula Albionum</i> has two possible origins. It either derives from a cognate of the Latin <i>albus</i> meaning white, a reference to the <a href="/wiki/White_cliffs_of_Dover" class="mw-redirect" title="White cliffs of Dover">white cliffs of Dover</a> (the only part of Britain visible from the European mainland)<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> or from the phrase the "island of the <i>Albiones</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> in the now lost <i><a href="/wiki/Massaliote_Periplus" title="Massaliote Periplus">Massaliote Periplus</a></i>, that is attested through <a href="/wiki/Avienus" title="Avienus">Avienus</a>\' <i>Ora Maritima</i><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> to which the former presumably served as a source. <i>Albion</i> is now applied to England in a more poetic capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Another romantic name for England is <a href="/wiki/Lloegyr" title="Lloegyr">Loegria</a>, related to the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> word for England, <i>Lloegr</i>, and made popular by its use in <a href="/wiki/Arthurian_legend" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthurian legend">Arthurian legend</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_England" title="History of England">History of England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_antiquity">Prehistory and antiquity</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Prehistoric Britain</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Sun shining through row of upright standing stones with other stones horizontally on the top." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/220px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/330px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/440px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> monument</div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest known evidence of human presence in the area now known as England was that of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_antecessor" title="Homo antecessor">Homo antecessor</a></i>, dating to approximately 780,000 years ago. The oldest proto-human bones discovered in England date from 500,000&#160;years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Modern humans are known to have inhabited the area during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> period, though permanent settlements were only established within the last 6,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\nAfter the last <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">ice age</a> only large mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woolly_rhinoceros" title="Woolly rhinoceros">woolly rhinoceros</a> remained. Roughly 11,000&#160;years ago, when the <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheets" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice sheets">ice sheets</a> began to recede, humans repopulated the area; genetic research suggests they came from the northern part of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The sea level was lower than now and Britain was connected by <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a> to Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\nAs the seas rose, it was separated from Ireland 10,000&#160;years ago and from Eurasia two millennia later.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Beaker_culture" title="Beaker culture">Beaker culture</a> arrived around 2,500&#160;BC, introducing drinking and food vessels constructed from clay, as well as vessels used as reduction pots to smelt copper ores.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> It was during this time that major <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> monuments such as <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avebury" title="Avebury">Avebury</a> were constructed. By heating together tin and copper, which were in abundance in the area, the Beaker culture people made <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, and later iron from iron ores. The development of iron <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> allowed the construction of better <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">ploughs</a>, advancing agriculture (for instance, with <a href="/wiki/Celtic_field" title="Celtic field">Celtic fields</a>), as well as the production of more effective weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of woman, with outstretched arm, in white dress with red cloak and helmet, with other human figures to her right and below her to the left." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg/170px-Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg/255px-Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="303" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Boudica_by_John_Opie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudica</a> led an uprising against the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic culture</a>, deriving from the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La T\xc3\xa8ne culture">La T\xc3\xa8ne cultures</a>, arrived from Central Europe. <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">Brythonic</a> was the spoken language during this time. Society was tribal; according to <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geographia" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographia">Geographia</a></i> there were around 20 tribes in the area. Earlier divisions are unknown because the Britons were not literate. Like other regions on the edge of the Empire, Britain had long enjoyed trading links with the Romans. Julius Caesar of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> attempted to <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar&#39;s invasions of Britain">invade twice</a> in 55&#160;BC; although largely unsuccessful, he managed to set up a <a href="/wiki/Roman_client_kingdoms_in_Britain" title="Roman client kingdoms in Britain">client king</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Trinovantes" title="Trinovantes">Trinovantes</a>.\n</p><p>The Romans invaded Britain in 43 AD during the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>, subsequently <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">conquering much of Britain</a>, and the area was incorporated into the Roman Empire as <a href="/wiki/Britannia_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Britannia province">Britannia province</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The best-known of the native tribes who attempted to resist were the <a href="/wiki/Catuvellauni" title="Catuvellauni">Catuvellauni</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Caratacus" title="Caratacus">Caratacus</a>. Later, an uprising led by <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudica</a>, Queen of the <a href="/wiki/Iceni" title="Iceni">Iceni</a>, ended with Boudica\'s suicide following her defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Watling_Street" title="Battle of Watling Street">Battle of Watling Street</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This era saw a <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> culture prevail with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman architecture">Roman architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_aqueducts_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="List of aqueducts in the Roman Empire">aqueducts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sanitation_in_ancient_Rome" title="Sanitation in ancient Rome">sewers</a>, many agricultural items and silk.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In the 3rd century, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> died at <a href="/wiki/Eboracum" title="Eboracum">Eboracum</a> (now <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>), where <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine</a> was subsequently proclaimed emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is debate about when Christianity was first introduced; it was no later than the 4th century, probably much earlier. According to <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>, missionaries were sent from Rome by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Eleuterus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pope Eleuterus">Eleutherius</a> at the request of the chieftain <a href="/wiki/Lucius_of_Britain" title="Lucius of Britain">Lucius of Britain</a> in 180 AD, to settle differences as to Eastern and Western ceremonials, which were disturbing the church. There are traditions linked to Glastonbury claiming an introduction through <a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Arimathea" title="Joseph of Arimathea">Joseph of Arimathea</a>, while others claim through <a href="/wiki/Lucius_of_Britain" title="Lucius of Britain">Lucius of Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> By 410, during the <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">Decline of the Roman Empire</a>, Britain was left exposed by the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">end of Roman rule in Britain</a> and the withdrawal of Roman army units, to defend the frontiers in continental Europe and partake in civil wars.<sup id="cite_ref-james_anglosaxons_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-james_anglosaxons-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Celtic Christian monastic and missionary movements flourished: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Patrick</a> (5th-century Ireland) and in the 6th century Brendan (Clonfert), Comgall (Bangor), David (Wales), Aiden (Lindisfarne) and Columba (Iona). This period of Christianity was influenced by ancient Celtic culture in its sensibilities, polity, practices and theology. Local "congregations" were centred in the monastic community and monastic leaders were more like chieftains, as peers, rather than in the more hierarchical system of the Roman-dominated church.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="England in the Middle Ages">England in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_replica_(face).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Studded and decorated metallic mask of human face." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg/220px-Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg/330px-Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg/440px-Sutton_Hoo_replica_%28face%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_replica_(face).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of the 7th-century ceremonial <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo_helmet" title="Sutton Hoo helmet">Sutton Hoo helmet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_East_Anglia" title="Kingdom of East Anglia">Kingdom of East Anglia</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military of ancient Rome">Roman military</a> withdrawals left Britain open to invasion by pagan, seafaring warriors from north-western continental Europe, chiefly the Saxons, <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> and Frisians who had long raided the coasts of the Roman province and began to settle, initially in the eastern part of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-james_anglosaxons_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-james_anglosaxons-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Their advance was contained for some decades after the Britons\' victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badon" title="Battle of Badon">Battle of Mount Badon</a>, but subsequently resumed, over-running the fertile lowlands of Britain and reducing the area under <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a> control to a series of separate enclaves in the more rugged country to the west by the end of the 6th century. Contemporary texts describing this period are extremely scarce, giving rise to its description as a <a href="/wiki/Dark_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark Age">Dark Age</a>. The nature and progression of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain</a> is consequently subject to considerable disagreement. Roman-dominated Christianity had, in general, disappeared from the conquered territories, but was reintroduced by missionaries from Rome led by <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine</a> from 597 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Disputes between the Roman- and Celtic-dominated forms of Christianity ended in victory for the Roman tradition at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Whitby" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Whitby">Council of Whitby</a> (664), which was ostensibly about haircuts and the date of Easter, but more significantly, about the differences in Roman and Celtic forms of authority, theology, and practice (Lehane).\n</p><p>During the settlement period the lands ruled by the incomers seem to have been fragmented into numerous tribal territories, but by the 7th century, when substantial evidence of the situation again becomes available, these had coalesced into roughly a dozen kingdoms including <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_East_Anglia" title="Kingdom of East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Essex" title="Kingdom of Essex">Essex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">Sussex</a>. Over the following centuries, this process of political consolidation continued.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The 7th century saw a struggle for hegemony between Northumbria and Mercia, which in the 8th century gave way to Mercian preeminence.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In the early 9th century Mercia was displaced as the foremost kingdom by Wessex. Later in that century escalating attacks by the <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danes</a> culminated in the conquest of the north and east of England, overthrowing the kingdoms of Northumbria, Mercia and East Anglia. Wessex under <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> was left as the only surviving English kingdom, and under his successors, it steadily expanded at the expense of the kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>. This brought about the political unification of England, first accomplished under <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan" title="\xc3\x86thelstan">\xc3\x86thelstan</a> in 927 and definitively established after further conflicts by <a href="/wiki/Eadred" title="Eadred">Eadred</a> in 953. A fresh wave of Scandinavian attacks from the late 10th century ended with the conquest of this united kingdom by <a href="/wiki/Sweyn_Forkbeard" title="Sweyn Forkbeard">Sweyn Forkbeard</a> in 1013 and again by his son <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a> in 1016, turning it into the centre of a short-lived <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Empire" title="North Sea Empire">North Sea Empire</a> that also included <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Denmark">Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>. However, the native royal dynasty was restored with the accession of <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> in 1042.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt,_1415.png" class="image"><img alt="King Henry V at the Battle of Agincourt, 1415." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png/200px-King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png" width="200" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png/300px-King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png/400px-King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_1415.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_V_at_the_Battle_of_Agincourt,_1415.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/King_Henry_V_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King Henry V of England">King Henry V</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a>, fought on <a href="/wiki/Saint_Crispin" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Crispin">Saint Crispin</a>\'s Day and concluded with an English victory against a larger French army in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A dispute over the succession to Edward led to the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> in 1066, accomplished by an army led by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">Duke William of Normandy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> themselves originated from <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and had settled in Normandy in the late 9th and early 10th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This conquest led to the almost total dispossession of the English elite and its replacement by a new French-speaking aristocracy, whose speech had a profound and permanent effect on the English language.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Subsequently, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</a> from Anjou inherited the English throne under <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a>, adding England to the budding <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a> of fiefs the family had inherited in France including <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bartlett_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bartlett-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> They reigned for three centuries, some noted monarchs being <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bartlett_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bartlett-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The period saw changes in trade and legislation, including the signing of the <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i>, an English legal charter used to limit the sovereign\'s powers by law and protect the privileges of freemen. Catholic <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a> flourished, providing philosophers, and the universities of Oxford and Cambridge were founded with royal patronage. The <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Wales" title="Principality of Wales">Principality of Wales</a> became a Plantagenet fief during the 13th century<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a> was given to the English monarchy by the Pope.\n</p><p>During the 14th century, the Plantagenets and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Valois" title="House of Valois">House of Valois</a> both claimed to be legitimate claimants to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Capet" title="House of Capet">House of Capet</a> and with it France; the two powers clashed in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> epidemic <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">hit England</a>; starting in 1348, it eventually killed up to half of England\'s <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">inhabitants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> From 1453 to 1487 civil war occurred between two branches of the royal family \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">Yorkists</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">Lancastrians</a> \xe2\x80\x93 known as the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Eventually it led to the Yorkists losing the throne entirely to a Welsh noble family the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">Tudors</a>, a branch of the Lancastrians headed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry Tudor</a> who invaded with Welsh and Breton mercenaries, gaining victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a> where the Yorkist king <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a> was killed.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern">Early Modern</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger,_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of large bearded man with fur-trimmed cloak, wearing a hat." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2994" data-file-height="4203" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger,_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">King Henry VIII</a> became <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Head_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Head of the Church of England">Supreme Head of the Church of England</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor period</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> reached England through Italian courtiers, who reintroduced artistic, educational and scholarly debate from classical antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> England began to develop <a href="/wiki/English_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="English Navy">naval skills</a>, and exploration to the West intensified.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> broke from communion with the Catholic Church, over issues relating to his divorce, under the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Supremacy" title="Acts of Supremacy">Acts of Supremacy</a> in 1534 which proclaimed the monarch head of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. In contrast with much of European Protestantism, the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">roots of the split</a> were more political than theological.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> He also legally incorporated his ancestral land Wales into the Kingdom of England with the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535%E2%80%931542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542">1535\xe2\x80\x931542 acts</a>. There were internal religious conflicts during the reigns of Henry\'s daughters, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>. The former took the country back to Catholicism while the latter broke from it again, forcefully asserting the supremacy of <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>.\n</p><p>Competing with <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spain</a>, the first English colony in the Americas was founded in 1585 by explorer <a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">Walter Raleigh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> and named <a href="/wiki/Roanoke_Colony" title="Roanoke Colony">Roanoke</a>. The Roanoke colony failed and is known as the lost colony after it was found abandoned on the return of the late-arriving supply ship.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>, England also competed with the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a> in the East. During the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan period">Elizabethan period</a>, England was at war with Spain. An <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">armada</a> sailed from Spain in 1588 as part of a wider plan to invade England and re-establish a Catholic monarchy. The plan was thwarted by bad coordination, stormy weather and successful harrying attacks by an English fleet under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Howard,_1st_Earl_of_Nottingham" title="Charles Howard, 1st Earl of Nottingham">Lord Howard of Effingham</a>. This failure did not end the threat: Spain launched two further armadas, in <a href="/wiki/2nd_Spanish_Armada" title="2nd Spanish Armada">1596</a> and <a href="/wiki/3rd_Spanish_Armada" title="3rd Spanish Armada">1597</a>, but both were driven back by storms. The political structure of the island changed in 1603, when the <a href="/wiki/King_of_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Scots">King of Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI</a>, a kingdom which had been a long-time rival to English interests, inherited the throne of England as <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James I</a>, thereby creating a <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">personal union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britons_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britons-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He styled himself <a href="/wiki/King_of_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Great Britain">King of Great Britain</a>, although this had no basis in English law.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Under the auspices of King James VI and I the Authorised <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Version</a> of the Holy Bible was published in 1611. It has not only been ranked with <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>\'s works as the greatest masterpiece of literature in the English language but also was the standard version of the Bible read by most Protestant Christians for four hundred years until modern revisions were produced in the 20th century.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of seated male figure, with long black hair wearing a white cape and breeches." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg/170px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg/255px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg/340px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3060" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright_or_studio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a> restored the monarchy under King <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles&#160;II</a> and peace after the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Based on conflicting political, religious and social positions, the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> was fought between the supporters of <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Parliament</a> and those of King <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, known colloquially as <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Roundheads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Cavaliers</a> respectively. This was an interwoven part of the wider multifaceted <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a>, involving <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>. The Parliamentarians were victorious, Charles I was executed and the kingdom replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a>. Leader of the Parliament forces, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> declared himself <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> in 1653; a period of <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">personal rule</a> followed.<sup id="cite_ref-Ocromwell_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ocromwell-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> After Cromwell\'s death and the resignation of his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a> as Lord Protector, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> was invited to return as monarch in 1660, in a move called the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688, it was constitutionally established that King and Parliament should rule together, though Parliament would have the real power. This was established with the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights</a> in 1689. Among the statutes set down were that the law could only be made by Parliament and could not be suspended by the King, also that the King could not impose taxes or raise an army without the prior approval of Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Also since that time, no British monarch has entered the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> when it is sitting, which is annually commemorated at the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a> by the British monarch when the doors of the House of Commons are slammed in the face of the monarch\'s messenger, symbolising the rights of Parliament and its independence from the monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> With the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in 1660, science was greatly encouraged.\n</p><p>In 1666 the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a> gutted the City of London but it was rebuilt shortly afterwards<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> with many significant buildings designed by Sir <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a>. In Parliament two factions had emerged \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_faction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political faction)">Whigs</a>. Though the Tories initially supported Catholic king <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a>, some of them, along with the Whigs, during the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Revolution of 1688</a> invited Dutch prince William of Orange to defeat James and ultimately to become <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William III of England</a>. Some English people, especially in the north, were <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobites</a> and continued to support James and his sons. After the parliaments of England and Scotland agreed,<sup id="cite_ref-unionwithscotland_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unionwithscotland-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> the two countries joined in <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">political union</a>, to create the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> in 1707.<sup id="cite_ref-Britons_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britons-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> To accommodate the union, institutions such as the law and national churches of each remained separate.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Modern_and_contemporary">Late Modern and contemporary</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A stone factory stands against a vivid blue sky, its reflection mirrored in the waters below." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg/170px-Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg/255px-Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg/340px-Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1066" data-file-height="1599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saltaire_New_Mill_Reflected.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saltaire" title="Saltaire">Saltaire</a>, West Yorkshire, is a model mill town from the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Under the newly formed Kingdom of Great Britain, output from the Royal Society and other <a href="/wiki/English_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="English Enlightenment">English initiatives</a> combined with the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> to create innovations in science and engineering, while the enormous growth in <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">British overseas trade</a> protected by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> paved the way for the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Domestically it drove the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, a period of profound change in the <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">socioeconomic</a> and cultural conditions of England, resulting in industrialised agriculture, manufacture, engineering and mining, as well as new and pioneering road, rail and water networks to facilitate their expansion and development.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The opening of Northwest England\'s <a href="/wiki/Bridgewater_Canal" title="Bridgewater Canal">Bridgewater Canal</a> in 1761 ushered in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_canal_system" title="History of the British canal system">canal age in Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton20015_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton20015-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In 1825 the world\'s first permanent steam locomotive-hauled passenger railway \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a> \xe2\x80\x93 opened to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton20015_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton20015-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McConnel_%26_Company_mills,_about_1820.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg/220px-McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg/330px-McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg/440px-McConnel_%26_Company_mills%2C_about_1820.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2268" data-file-height="1258" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McConnel_%26_Company_mills,_about_1820.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">Cotton mills</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, the world\'s "first industrial city", circa 1820.<sup id="cite_ref-Industrial_city_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Industrial_city-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>During the Industrial Revolution, many workers moved from England\'s countryside to new and expanding urban industrial areas to work in factories, for instance at <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, dubbed "Workshop of the World" and "Warehouse City" respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> England maintained relative stability throughout the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>; <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a> was British Prime Minister for the reign of <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> planned to <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_planned_invasion_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Napoleon&#39;s planned invasion of the United Kingdom">invade from the south-east</a>. However this failed to manifest and the Napoleonic forces were defeated by the British at sea by <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson" title="Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson">Lord Nelson</a> and on land by the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>. The Napoleonic Wars fostered a concept of <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">Britishness</a> and a united national <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British people</a>, shared with the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> and Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A cuboid granite cenotaph." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg/170px-UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg/255px-UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg/340px-UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5000" data-file-height="6348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK-2014-London-The_Cenotaph.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Cenotaph,_Whitehall" title="The Cenotaph, Whitehall">The Cenotaph, Whitehall</a>, is a memorial to members of the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> who died during the two World Wars</div></div></div>\n<p>London became the largest and most populous metropolitan area in the world during the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, and trade within the British Empire \xe2\x80\x93 as well as the standing of the British military and navy \xe2\x80\x93 was prestigious.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Political agitation at home from radicals such as the <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffragettes</a> enabled legislative reform and <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Power shifts in east-central Europe led to World War I; hundreds of thousands of English soldiers died fighting for the United Kingdom as part of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup> Two decades later, in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the United Kingdom was again one of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a>. At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Phoney_War" title="Phoney War">Phoney War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> became the wartime Prime Minister. Developments in warfare technology saw many cities damaged by air-raids during <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>. Following the war, the British Empire experienced rapid <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a>, and there was a speeding up of technological innovations; automobiles became the primary means of transport and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Whittle" title="Frank Whittle">Frank Whittle</a>\'s development of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engine</a> led to wider <a href="/wiki/Air_travel" title="Air travel">air travel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Residential patterns were altered in England by private motoring, and by the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service</a> (NHS) in 1948. The UK\'s NHS provided <a href="/wiki/Publicly_funded_health_care" title="Publicly funded health care">publicly funded health care</a> to all UK permanent residents free at the point of need, being paid for from general taxation. Combined, these changes prompted the reform of <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local government in England</a> in the mid-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the 20th century there has been significant population movement to England, mostly from other parts of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, but also from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Since the 1970s there has been a large move away from manufacturing and an increasing emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Service_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Service industry">service industry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thatcher_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> As part of the United Kingdom, the area joined a <a href="/wiki/Common_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Common market">common market</a> initiative called the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> which became the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. Since the late 20th century the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Politics of the United Kingdom">administration of the United Kingdom</a> has moved towards <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved governance</a> in Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> continues to exist as a jurisdiction within the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Wales_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Wales-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Devolution has stimulated a greater emphasis on a more English-specific identity and patriotism.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward180_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward180-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> There is no devolved English government, but an attempt to create a similar system on a sub-regional basis was rejected by referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-refreject_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refreject-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_England" title="Politics of England">Politics of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of rectangular floodlight building, reflected in water. The building has multiple towers including one at each end. The tower on the right includes an illuminated clock face." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg/220px-HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg/330px-HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg/440px-HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5856" data-file-height="3134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HOUSES_OF_PARLIAMENT_DSC_7057_pano_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>, the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As part of the United Kingdom, the basic political system in England is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> There has not been a <a href="/wiki/Government_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of England">government of England</a> since 1707, when the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>, putting into effect the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>, joined England and Scotland to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unionwithscotland_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unionwithscotland-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Before the union England was ruled by <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">its monarch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>. Today England is governed directly by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>, although other <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a> have <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> governments.<sup id="cite_ref-Devolution_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devolution-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> which is the <a href="/wiki/Lower_house" title="Lower house">lower house</a> of the British Parliament based at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>, there are 532 Members of Parliament (MPs) for constituencies in England, out of the 650 total.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">United Kingdom general election, 2017</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> won 317 seats (the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Commons_(United_Kingdom)" title="Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)">Speaker of the House</a> not being counted as a Conservative), more than any other party, though not enough to achieve an overall majority. The Conservative party, headed by the prime minister <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, won 55 more seats than the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> (Scotland only) won 35 out of 59 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Westminster_constituencies" title="Scottish Westminster constituencies">Scottish seats in the House of Commons</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Lines of men wearing large black bearskin hats and red tunics." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg/220px-Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg/330px-Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg/440px-Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2115" data-file-height="1354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buck.palace.soldiers.arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Changing of the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Guard" title="Queen&#39;s Guard">Queen\'s Guard</a> at the <a href="/wiki/British_monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarch">royal residence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As the United Kingdom is a member of the European Union, there are elections held regionally in England to decide who is sent as <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_European_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of the European Parliament">Members of the European Parliament</a>. The <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2014 (United Kingdom)">2014 European Parliament election</a> saw the regions of England elect the following MEPs: 22 <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> (UKIP), 17 Conservatives, 17 Labour, 3 <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Greens</a>, and one Liberal Democrat.\n</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a>, in which other countries of the United Kingdom \xe2\x80\x93 Scotland, Wales and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> \xe2\x80\x93 each have their own devolved parliament or assemblies for local issues, there has been debate about how to counterbalance this in England. Originally it was planned that various <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regions of England</a> would be devolved, but following the proposal\'s rejection by the <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East</a> in a referendum, this has not been carried out.<sup id="cite_ref-refreject_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refreject-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One major issue is the <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian_question" title="West Lothian question">West Lothian question</a>, in which MPs from Scotland and Wales are able to vote on legislation affecting only England, while English MPs have no equivalent right to legislate on devolved matters.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> This when placed in the context of England being the only country of the United Kingdom not to have free cancer treatment, prescriptions, residential care for the elderly and <a href="/wiki/Top-up_fees" class="mw-redirect" title="Top-up fees">free top-up university fees</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-msnmoney_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnmoney-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> has led to a steady rise in <a href="/wiki/English_nationalism" title="English nationalism">English nationalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Some have suggested the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Devolved_English_parliament" title="Devolved English parliament">devolved English parliament</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> while others have proposed simply limiting voting on legislation which only affects England to English MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Ornate grey stone building." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/220px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/330px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/440px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Courts_of_Justice" title="Royal Courts of Justice">Royal Courts of Justice</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> legal system, developed over the centuries, is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> legal systems used in most <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> countries<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and the United States (except <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>). Despite now being part of the United Kingdom, the legal system of the <a href="/wiki/Courts_of_England_and_Wales" title="Courts of England and Wales">Courts of England and Wales</a> continued, under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>, as a separate legal system from the one used in Scotland. The general essence of English law is that it is made by judges sitting in courts, applying their common sense and knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Legal_precedent" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal precedent">legal precedent</a> \xe2\x80\x93 <i><a href="/wiki/Stare_decisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Stare decisis">stare decisis</a></i> \xe2\x80\x93 to the facts before them.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Courts_of_England_and_Wales" title="Courts of England and Wales">court system</a> is headed by the Senior Courts of England and Wales, consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Appeal_of_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of Appeal of England and Wales">Court of Appeal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a> for civil cases, and the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Court" title="Crown Court">Crown Court</a> for criminal cases.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Supreme Court of the United Kingdom">Supreme Court of the United Kingdom</a> is the highest court for criminal and civil cases in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>. It was created in 2009 after constitutional changes, taking over the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_functions_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Judicial functions of the House of Lords">judicial functions of the House of Lords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> A decision of the Supreme Court is binding on every other court in the hierarchy, which must follow its directions.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crime increased between 1981 and 1995 but fell by 42% in the period 1995\xe2\x80\x932006.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The prison population doubled over the same period, giving it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_incarceration_rate" title="List of countries by incarceration rate">highest incarceration rate</a> in Western Europe at 147 per 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-crimebbc_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crimebbc-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Prison_Service" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Prison Service">Her Majesty\'s Prison Service</a>, reporting to the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Justice_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Justice (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Justice</a>, manages most <a href="/wiki/Prisons_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisons in England">prisons</a>, housing over 85,000 convicts.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Regions.2C_counties.2C_and_districts"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions,_counties,_and_districts">Regions, counties, and districts</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_England" title="Subdivisions of England">Subdivisions of England</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">Regions of England</a>, <a
