tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglospeak.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/220px-Anglospeak.svg.png" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/330px-Anglospeak.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/440px-Anglospeak.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglospeak.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Majority native language</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #8ddada; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Official, but not majority language</div><span style="font-size:85%;">Note: English is also an <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">official language of the EU</a></span><sup id="cite_ref-EUlang_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUlang-205">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As its name suggests, the English language, today spoken by hundreds of millions of people around the world, originated as the language of England, where it remains the principal tongue spoken by 98% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-CensusEnglish_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CensusEnglish-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> It is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> language in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> family.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest of England">Norman conquest</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> language was displaced and confined to the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">lower social classes</a> as <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> were used by the aristocracy.\n</p><p>By the 15th century, English was back in fashion among all classes, though much changed; the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> form showed many signs of French influence, both in vocabulary and spelling. During the <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a>, many words were coined from Latin and Greek origins.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> has extended this custom of flexibility when it comes to incorporating words from different languages. Thanks in large part to the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, the English language is the world\'s unofficial <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/English_as_a_second_or_foreign_language" title="English as a second or foreign language">English language learning and teaching</a> is an important <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic activity</a>, and includes <a href="/wiki/Language_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Language schools">language schooling</a>, tourism spending, and publishing. There is no <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_legislation" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom legislation">legislation</a> mandating an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> for England,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> but English is the only language used for official business. Despite the country\'s relatively small size, there are many distinct <a href="/wiki/Regional_accents_of_English_speakers#England" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional accents of English speakers">regional accents</a>, and individuals with particularly strong accents may not be easily understood everywhere in the country.\n</p><p>As well as English, England has two other <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">indigenous languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>. Cornish died out as a community language in the 18th century but is being revived,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and is now protected under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> It is spoken by 0.1% of people in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> and is taught to some degree in several primary and secondary schools.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the modern border between Wales and England was established by the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542</a>, many Welsh-speaking communities found themselves on the English side of the border. Welsh was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Archenfield" title="Archenfield">Archenfield</a> in Herefordshire into the nineteenth century,<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> and by natives of parts of western <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> until the middle of the twentieth century if not later.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">State schools</a> teach students a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>, usually French, German or Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Due to immigration, it was reported in 2007 that around 800,000 school students spoke a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a> at home,<sup id="cite_ref-Paton_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paton-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> the most common being <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>. However, following the 2011 census data released by the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a>, figures now show that Polish is the main language spoken in England after English.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="Large yellow stone ornate building with buttresses and square central tower." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/220px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/330px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/440px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3113" data-file-height="2395" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>, seat of the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_England" title="Religion in England">Religion in England</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Christianity in England">History of Christianity in England</a></div>\n<p>In the 2011 census, 59.4% of the population of England specified their religion as Christian, 24.7% answered that they had no religion, 5% specified that they were <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>, while 3.7% of the population belongs to other religions and 7.2% did not give an answer.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Christianity is the most widely practised religion in England, as it has been since the Early Middle Ages, although it was first introduced much earlier in Gaelic and Roman times. This <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic Church">Celtic Church</a> was gradually joined to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Catholic</a> hierarchy following the 6th-century <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">St&#160;Augustine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a> of England is the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> which left communion with <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Rome</a> in the 1530s when <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry&#160;VIII</a> was unable to annul his divorce to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">the aunt</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">king of Spain</a>. The church regards itself as both Catholic and <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorge-dragon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stgeorge-dragon.jpg/170px-Stgeorge-dragon.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stgeorge-dragon.jpg/255px-Stgeorge-dragon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stgeorge-dragon.jpg/340px-Stgeorge-dragon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2859" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorge-dragon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of England</div></div></div>\n<p>There are <a href="/wiki/High_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="High Church">High Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Low_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Low Church">Low Church</a> traditions and some Anglicans regard themselves as <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholics">Anglo-Catholics</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Tractarian_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Tractarian movement">Tractarian movement</a>. The monarch of the United Kingdom is the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a>, which has around 26&#160;million baptised members (of whom the vast majority are not regular churchgoers). It forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> acting as its symbolic worldwide head.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_cathedrals_in_England_and_Wales" title="List of cathedrals in England and Wales">cathedrals</a> and parish churches are historic buildings of significant architectural importance, such as <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>.\n</p><p>The 2nd-largest Christian practice is the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Rite" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin Rite">Latin Rite</a> of the Catholic Church. Since its reintroduction after the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a>, the Church has organised ecclesiastically on an <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church_in_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church in England and Wales">England and Wales</a> basis where there are 4.5&#160;million members (most of whom are English).<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> There has been one Pope from England to date, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Adrian_IV" title="Pope Adrian IV">Adrian IV</a>; while saints <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a> are regarded as <a href="/wiki/Doctors_of_the_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctors of the Church">Doctors of the Church</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/220px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/330px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/440px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> is a notable example of <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">coronation of the British monarch</a> traditionally takes place at the Abbey</div></div></div>\n<p>A form of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain" title="Methodist Church of Great Britain">Methodism</a> is the third largest Christian practice and grew out of Anglicanism through <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> It gained popularity in the <a href="/wiki/Mill_town" title="Mill town">mill towns</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, and amongst tin miners in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> There are other <a href="/wiki/Non-conformist" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-conformist">non-conformist</a> minorities, such as <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Salvation_Army" title="The Salvation Army">The Salvation Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The patron saint of England is <a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a>; his symbolic cross is included in the flag of England, as well as in the Union Flag as part of a combination.<sup id="cite_ref-flaghistory_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flaghistory-229">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> There are many other English and associated saints; some of the best-known are: <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_the_Martyr" title="Edmund the Martyr">Edmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Alban" title="Saint Alban">Alban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aidan_of_Lindisfarne" title="Aidan of Lindisfarne">Aidan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petroc" title="Saint Petroc">Petroc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran" title="Saint Piran">Piran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Clitherow" title="Margaret Clitherow">Margaret Clitherow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a>. There are non-Christian religions practised. Jews have a history of a small minority on the island since 1070.<sup id="cite_ref-jews_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jews-230">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> They were expelled from England in 1290 following the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">Edict of Expulsion</a>, only to be allowed back in 1656.<sup id="cite_ref-jews_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jews-230">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg/250px-Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg" width="250" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg/375px-Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg/500px-Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2223" data-file-height="1631" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neasden_Temple_-_Shree_Swaminarayan_Hindu_Mandir_-_Gate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/BAPS_Shri_Swaminarayan_Mandir_London" title="BAPS Shri Swaminarayan Mandir London">BAPS Shri Swaminarayan Mandir in London, United Kingdom</a> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temple</a> in England.</div></div></div> \n<p>Especially since the 1950s, religions from the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">former British colonies</a> have grown in numbers, due to immigration. Islam is the most common of these, now accounting for around 5% of the population in England.<sup id="cite_ref-statsio_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statsio-231">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> are next in number, adding up to 2.8% combined,<sup id="cite_ref-statsio_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statsio-231">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> introduced from India and <a href="/wiki/South_East_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="South East Asia">South East Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-statsio_231-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statsio-231">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A small minority of the population practise ancient <a href="/wiki/Pagan_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan religion">Pagan religions</a>. <a href="/wiki/Neopaganism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Neopaganism in the United Kingdom">Neopaganism in the United Kingdom</a> is primarily represented by <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Witchcraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Contemporary Witchcraft">Witchcraft religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Druidry" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Druidry">Druidry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_neopaganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic neopaganism">Heathenry</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 UK Census</a>, there are roughly 53,172 people who identify as Pagan in England,<sup id="cite_ref-pagan_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pagan-233">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup> and 3,448 in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pagan_233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pagan-233">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup> including 11,026 <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wiccans</a> in England and 740 in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;nb 6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_England" title="Education in England">Education in England</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_England" title="List of universities in England">List of universities in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Warwick_School_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Warwick_School_front.jpg/200px-Warwick_School_front.jpg" width="200" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Warwick_School_front.jpg/300px-Warwick_School_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Warwick_School_front.jpg/400px-Warwick_School_front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2920" data-file-height="2152" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Warwick_School_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The frontage of <a href="/wiki/Warwick_School" title="Warwick School">Warwick School</a>, one of the oldest independent schools in England</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Education" title="Department for Education">Department for Education</a> is the government department responsible for issues affecting people in England up to the age of 19, including education.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> State-run and state-funded schools are attended by approximately 93% of English schoolchildren.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Of these, a minority are <a href="/wiki/Faith_school" title="Faith school">faith schools</a> (primarily <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> or <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> schools). Children who are between the ages of 3 and 5 attend nursery or an <a href="/wiki/Early_Years_Foundation_Stage" title="Early Years Foundation Stage">Early Years Foundation Stage</a> reception unit within a primary school. Children between the ages of 5 and 11 attend primary school, and secondary school is attended by those aged between 11 and 16. After finishing compulsory education, students take <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education" title="General Certificate of Secondary Education">GCSE</a> examinations. Students may then opt to continue into <a href="/wiki/Further_education" title="Further education">further education</a> for two years. <a href="/wiki/List_of_further_education_colleges_in_England" title="List of further education colleges in England">Further education colleges</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form_colleges" class="mw-redirect" title="Sixth form colleges">sixth form colleges</a>) often form part of a secondary school site. <a href="/wiki/A-level" class="mw-redirect" title="A-level">A-level</a> examinations are sat by a large number of further education students, and often form the basis of an application to university.\n</p><p>Although most English secondary schools are <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_school" title="Comprehensive school">comprehensive</a>, in some areas there are selective intake <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a>, to which entrance is subject to passing the <a href="/wiki/Eleven-plus" title="Eleven-plus">eleven-plus</a> exam. Around 7.2% of English schoolchildren attend <a href="/wiki/Independent_school_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent school (UK)">private schools</a>, which are funded by private sources.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Standards in state schools are monitored by the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_Standards_in_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Office for Standards in Education">Office for Standards in Education</a>, and in private schools by the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Schools_Inspectorate" title="Independent Schools Inspectorate">Independent Schools Inspectorate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/200px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" width="200" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/300px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/400px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png 2x" data-file-width="832" data-file-height="693" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a>, University of Oxford</div></div></div>\n<p>Higher education students normally attend university from age 18 onwards, where they study for an <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">academic degree</a>. There are over 90&#160;universities in England, all but one of which are <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public institutions</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Business,_Innovation_and_Skills" title="Department for Business, Innovation and Skills">Department for Business, Innovation and Skills</a> is the government department responsible for higher education in England.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Students are generally entitled to <a href="/wiki/Student_loan" title="Student loan">student loans</a> to cover the cost of <a href="/wiki/Tuition_fees" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuition fees">tuition fees</a> and living costs.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;nb 7&#93;</a></sup> The first degree offered to undergraduates is the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor&#39;s degree">Bachelor\'s degree</a>, which usually takes three years to complete. Students are then able to work towards a postgraduate degree, which usually takes one year, or towards a doctorate, which takes three or more years. Since the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Bedford_College,_London" title="Bedford College, London">Bedford College</a> (<a href="/wiki/University_of_London" title="University of London">London</a>), <a href="/wiki/Girton_College,_Cambridge" title="Girton College, Cambridge">Girton College</a> (Cambridge) and <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a> (Oxford) in the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a>, women also can obtain a university degree.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_England" title="List of universities in England">England\'s universities</a> include some of the highest-ranked universities in the world; <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College London</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a> and <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King&#39;s College London">King\'s College London</a> are all ranked in the global top 30 in the 2018 <i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> has been described as the world\'s leading social science institution for both teaching and research.<sup id="cite_ref-london_156_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-london_156-242">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/London_Business_School" title="London Business School">London Business School</a> is considered one of the world\'s leading business schools and in 2010 its MBA programme was ranked best in the world by the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ft_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft-243">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">Academic degrees</a> in England are usually split into classes: first class (1st), upper second class (2:1), lower second class (2:2), third (3rd), and unclassified.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_School,_Canterbury" title="The King&#39;s School, Canterbury">The King\'s School, Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/King%27s_School,_Rochester" title="King&#39;s School, Rochester">King\'s School, Rochester</a> are the oldest schools in the English-speaking world.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Many of England\'s most well-known schools, such as <a href="/wiki/Winchester_College" title="Winchester College">Winchester College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_School_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="St Paul&#39;s School (London)">St Paul\'s School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrow_School" title="Harrow School">Harrow School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_School" title="Rugby School">Rugby School</a> are fee-paying institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_England" title="Culture of England">Culture of England</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Pauls_Cathedral,_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="White stone building with tower topped with a dome. In the foreground are trees and a red rectangular vertical box with windows." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg/170px-St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg/255px-St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg/340px-St_Pauls_Cathedral%2C_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Pauls_Cathedral,_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Red_telephone_box" title="Red telephone box">red telephone box</a> in front of <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul\'s Cathedral</a>, one of the most important buildings of the <a href="/wiki/English_Baroque" title="English Baroque">English Baroque</a> period</div></div></div>\n<p>Many ancient <a href="/wiki/Standing_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Standing stone">standing stone</a> monuments were erected during the prehistoric period; amongst the best known are <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Arrows" title="Devil&#39;s Arrows">Devil\'s Arrows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudston_Monolith" title="Rudston Monolith">Rudston Monolith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Castlerigg_stone_circle" title="Castlerigg stone circle">Castlerigg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> With the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Ancient Roman architecture</a> there was a development of <a href="/wiki/Basilicas" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilicas">basilicas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_baths" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman baths">baths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphitheaters" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphitheaters">amphitheaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_arch" title="Triumphal arch">triumphal arches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">Roman temples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_road" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman road">Roman roads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman fort">Roman forts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockade" title="Stockade">stockades</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(bridge)" title="Aqueduct (bridge)">aqueducts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-roman_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roman-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> It was the Romans who founded the first cities and towns such as London, Bath, York, Chester and St Albans. Perhaps the best-known example is <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian\'s Wall</a> stretching right across northern England.<sup id="cite_ref-roman_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roman-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Another well-preserved example is the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Baths_(Bath)" title="Roman Baths (Bath)">Roman Baths</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath, Somerset</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-roman_247-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roman-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_architecture" title="Anglo-Saxon architecture">Early Medieval architecture\'s</a> secular buildings were simple constructions mainly using timber with <a href="/wiki/Thatch" class="mw-redirect" title="Thatch">thatch</a> for roofing. Ecclesiastical architecture ranged from a synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian Ireland">Hiberno</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Germanic_Christianity" title="Germanic Christianity">Saxon</a> <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian_art_and_architecture" title="Early Christian art and architecture">Early Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> and architecture characterised by pilaster-strips, blank arcading, baluster shafts and triangular headed openings. After the Norman conquest in 1066 various <a href="/wiki/Castles_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Castles in England">Castles in England</a> were created so law lords could uphold their authority and in the north to protect from invasion. Some of the best-known medieval castles are the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Castle" title="Warwick Castle">Warwick Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durham_Castle" title="Durham Castle">Durham Castle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-buildings_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buildings-250">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A castle of square plan surrounded by a water-filled moat. It has round corner towers and a forbidding appearance." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg/220px-Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg/330px-Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg/440px-Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1348" data-file-height="899" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bodiam-castle-10My8-1197.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bodiam_Castle" title="Bodiam Castle">Bodiam Castle</a> is a 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Moat" title="Moat">moated</a> <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a> near <a href="/wiki/Robertsbridge" title="Robertsbridge">Robertsbridge</a> in East Sussex</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the Plantagenet era, an <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a> flourished, with prime examples including the <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_medieval_cathedrals_of_England" title="Architecture of the medieval cathedrals of England">medieval cathedrals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-buildings_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buildings-250">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Expanding on the <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman base</a> there was also <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palace" title="Palace">palaces</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_historic_houses_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="List of historic houses in England">great houses</a>, universities and <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish churches</a>. Medieval architecture was completed with the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Tudor_architecture" title="Tudor architecture">Tudor style</a>; the four-centred arch, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_arch" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor arch">Tudor arch</a>, was a defining feature as were <a href="/wiki/Wattle_and_daub" title="Wattle and daub">wattle and daub</a> houses domestically. In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> a form of architecture echoing classical antiquity synthesised with Christianity appeared, the <a href="/wiki/English_Baroque" title="English Baroque">English Baroque</a> style of architect <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a> being particularly championed.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian architecture</a> followed in a more refined style, evoking a simple Palladian form; the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Crescent" title="Royal Crescent">Royal Crescent</a> at Bath is one of the best examples of this. With the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romanticism</a> during Victorian period, a <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic Revival">Gothic Revival</a> was launched. In addition to this, around the same time the Industrial Revolution paved the way for buildings such as <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a>. Since the 1930s various <a href="/wiki/Modernist_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernist architecture">modernist</a> forms have appeared whose reception is often controversial, though traditionalist resistance movements continue with support in influential places.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;nb 8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folklore">Folklore</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_folklore" title="English folklore">English folklore</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" class="image"><img alt="Drawing depicting Robin Hood, wearing Lincoln green clothing, and Sir Guy of Gisbourne, wearing brown furs, in a forest preparing to shoot with bows and arrows." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png/170px-Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" width="170" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png/255px-Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png 2x" data-file-width="317" data-file-height="466" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> illustrated in 1912 wearing <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_green" title="Lincoln green">Lincoln green</a></div></div></div>\n<p>English folklore developed over many centuries. Some of the characters and stories are present across England, but most belong to specific regions. Common folkloric beings include <a href="/wiki/Pixie" title="Pixie">pixies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Giant (mythology)">giants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elf" title="Elf">elves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogeymen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bogeymen">bogeymen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troll" title="Troll">trolls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goblin" title="Goblin">goblins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_(mythology)" title="Dwarf (mythology)">dwarves</a>. While many legends and folk-customs are thought to be ancient, for instance the tales featuring <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Angel" title="Offa of Angel">Offa of Angel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayland_the_Smith" title="Wayland the Smith">Wayland the Smith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-keary_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keary-254">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> others date from after the Norman invasion; <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Merry_Men" title="Merry Men">Merry Men</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Forest" title="Sherwood Forest">Sherwood</a> and their battles with the <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_of_Nottingham" title="Sheriff of Nottingham">Sheriff of Nottingham</a> being, perhaps, the best known.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> tales originating from Brythonic traditions entered English folklore and developed into the <a href="/wiki/Arthurian_myth" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthurian myth">Arthurian myth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-woodbbc_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woodbbc-256">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-higham1_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-higham1-257">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> These were derived from <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a>, Welsh and French sources,<sup id="cite_ref-higham1_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-higham1-257">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> featuring <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camelot" title="Camelot">Camelot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excalibur" title="Excalibur">Excalibur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merlin" title="Merlin">Merlin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Round_Table" title="Knights of the Round Table">Knights of the Round Table</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lancelot" title="Lancelot">Lancelot</a>. These stories are most centrally brought together within <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i> (<i>History of the Kings of Britain</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;nb 9&#93;</a></sup> Another early figure from <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historic)">British tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Cole" class="mw-redirect" title="King Cole">King Cole</a>, may have been based on a real figure from Sub-Roman Britain. Many of the tales and <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">pseudo-histories</a> make up part of the wider <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>, a collection of shared British folklore.\n</p><p>Some folk figures are based on semi or actual historical people whose story has been passed down centuries; <a href="/wiki/Lady_Godiva" title="Lady Godiva">Lady Godiva</a> for instance was said to have ridden naked on horseback through <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hereward_the_Wake" title="Hereward the Wake">Hereward the Wake</a> was a heroic English figure resisting the Norman invasion, <a href="/wiki/Herne_the_Hunter" title="Herne the Hunter">Herne the Hunter</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> ghost associated with <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a> Forest and <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Great_Park" title="Windsor Great Park">Great Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mother_Shipton" title="Mother Shipton">Mother Shipton</a> is the archetypal witch.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> On 5 November people make bonfires, set off <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> and eat <a href="/wiki/Toffee_apple" class="mw-redirect" title="Toffee apple">toffee apples</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes_Night" title="Guy Fawkes Night">commemoration</a> of the foiling of the <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_Plot" title="Gunpowder Plot">Gunpowder Plot</a> centred on <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes" title="Guy Fawkes">Guy Fawkes</a>. The chivalrous bandit, such as <a href="/wiki/Dick_Turpin" title="Dick Turpin">Dick Turpin</a>, is a recurring character, while <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a> is the archetypal pirate. There are various national and regional folk activities, participated in to this day, such as <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">Morris dancing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maypole_dance" class="mw-redirect" title="Maypole dance">Maypole dancing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapper_sword" title="Rapper sword">Rapper sword</a> in the North East, <a href="/wiki/Long_Sword_dance" title="Long Sword dance">Long Sword dance</a> in Yorkshire, <a href="/wiki/Mummers_Play" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers Play">Mummers Plays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottle-kicking" title="Bottle-kicking">bottle-kicking</a> in Leicestershire, and <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_Hill_Cheese-Rolling_and_Wake" title="Cooper&#39;s Hill Cheese-Rolling and Wake">cheese-rolling</a> at <a href="/wiki/Brockworth,_Gloucestershire" title="Brockworth, Gloucestershire">Cooper\'s Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> There is no official national costume, but a few are well established such as the <a href="/wiki/Pearly_Kings_and_Queens" title="Pearly Kings and Queens">Pearly Kings and Queens</a> associated with cockneys, the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Guard" title="Queen&#39;s Guard">Royal Guard</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Morris_dance" title="Morris dance">Morris costume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beefeater" class="mw-redirect" title="Beefeater">Beefeaters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_cuisine" title="English cuisine">English cuisine</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:171px;max-width:171px"><div class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2418" data-file-height="1657" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Apple_pie" title="Apple pie">Apple pie</a> has been consumed in England since the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:238px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Fry.jpg" class="image"><img alt=""
